BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. THE SECTOR
Background
1.01       Brazil's total population, as reported in the 1970 and 1980 Cen-
suses, grew at an annual rate of 2.5%, from 93 to 119 million.  The urban
population grew from 52 to 80 million, or at 4.4% p.a. In 1980, 67% of the
population  lived  in urban areas.   The Brazilian organization of urban
areas, based on administrative/political considerations, extends to small
communities which in many other countries would be considered as rural.
Population projections for 1981-1990 show an urban growth rate of 3.5%
p.a., reaching 113 million at the end of the period.  The regions with the
fastest growing urban population are expected to be the North and Center-
West (over 5% p.a.), followed by the Northeast (3.8%), the Southeast
(3.2%), and the South (2.6%).
1.02        Water supply service levels increased notably between 1970 and
1980. The share of total population connected to public services rose from
30% to 51%.  The increase in sewerage service levels was also significant,
though limited to selected metropolitan areas.  The percentage of popula-
tion connected to sewerage rose from 11% to 24% (paras. 1.03 and 1.04).
The most complete and consistent sets of data on service levels are in the
Censuses, which group services according to quality standards.   For the
purposes of this report, service levels refer to houses connected to a
public system, even though individual systems are a viable alternative in
many areas.
1.03      Based on 1978 Pan American Health Organization (PAHO) statistics,
Brazil ranked eleventh among 21 countries in the region in terms of water
services coverage and thirteenth in sewerage services.  The total popula-
tion with water connections increased from 28 million people in 1970 to 59
million people in 1980. The service level in urban areas rose from 51% to
74%.   The  rural  service  level remained constant  at about one million
people, or 3% of the rural population. This apparent stagnation is partly
due to a more inclusive definition of urban areas in the 1980 census. Some
states actually experienced a decline in the rural service level, perhaps
indicating that well-served villages were reclassified from rural to
urban.  Water supply service levels in 1980 varied widely by regions: in
the North the level was 69%; in the Northeast, 59%; in the Southeast, 82%;
in the South, 75%; and in the Center-West, 57%. The states with the lowest
service levels in 1970 experienced the greatest advance during the decade.
1.04       Total population connected to sewerage increased from 10 million
people in 1970 to 28 million people in 1980.  The service level in urban
areas rose from 20% to 35%.  In 1980, 20% of the urban population had sep-
tic tanks and 36%, some other form of sanitation. Rural areas continued to



-2 -
be poorly served: 56% of the population had no sanitation systems and the
remaining population had rudimentary sanitation facilities. Sewerage serv-
ice levels in 1980 varied widely by region: in the North the level was 8%;
in the Northeast, 9%; in the Southeast, 54%; in the South, 18%; and in the
Center-West, 16%.
1.05       In 1980, Brazilian life expectancy at birth was 63 years and the
infant mortality rate was 77 per 1,000 live births. 1/ Analysis of Sao
Paulo data for 1950-79 concluded that income was the more important deter-
minant of infant mortality up to 1974. But after 1974, the 40% increase in
water supply service levels was estimated to have been the major cause of a
27% drop in the infant mortality rate. 2/ Enteritis and diahrreal diseases
constitute the major sanitation-related cause of mortality. These diseases
together with cholera, typhoid and dysentery accounted in 1977 for 28% of
all deaths in Maceio and 23% in Natal (Rio Grande do Norte). Their contri-
bution to the death rate was highest in the capitals of the Northeast (10%
to 28%) and the North (9% to 23%) and lowest in the South (2% to 11%).
Sanitation improvements in the North and Northeast can thus be expected to
provide relatively high benefits in terms of public health.
Sector Policies and Institutions
1.06      During the 1960s, a scarcity of financial resources and the weak-
ness of sector institutions caused the water supply and sanitation sector
in Brazil to lag behind other countries at comparable stages of develop-
ment.   Toward the end of this period,  the Government created the Banco
Nacional da Habitacao (BNH), and its major source of funds, the Fondo de
Garantia do Tempo de Servico (FGTS).   In 1968, the Sistema Financeiro de
Saneamento (SFS) was created and the first sanitation investments by BNH
were made. BNH became the central agency in the sector; revolving invest-
ment funds (FAEs) were set up at the state level and State Water Companies
(SWCs)  were  created.    The  Plano  Nacional  de  Saneamento  (PLANASA)  was
launched in 1970 and has remained basically unchanged since its creation,
although its operational targets have been modified and new programs for
small communities and the SWCs' institutional development have been added.
1.07      In 1978, PLANASA coverage was estimated at 70% of the urban popu-
lation served with water supply and 65% of the population with sewerage
services.   By 1981,  65% of all cities with over 5,000 inhabitants  had
joined PLANASA.   Most municipalities not joining PLANASA are relatively
well-off localities which do not want to relinquish control of their sys-
tems to the SWCs.   As the need for new investments grows, many of these
localities will probably join PLANASA as it represents an attractive source
of financing for sanitation investments.
1.08      The Ministry of Health operates in the sector through the Public
Health Service Foundation with responsibility for 600 water systems in
small towns, and a program (PIASS) for the promotion of sanitation activi-
ties in dispersed rural populations.
1/  World Development Report, 1982, The World Bank.
2/  Carlos Augusto Monteiro, "Contribuicao para o Estudo do Significado da
Evolucao do Coeficiente de Mortalidade Infantil do Municipio de Sao
Paulo nas tres ultimas Decadas (1950-1979)", Revista de Saude Publica,
16: 7-18, 1982.



-3-
The National Sanitation Plan
1.09       PLANASA is essentially a national system for planning and finan-
cing activities in the sanitation sector.   BNH is the central institution
and the main source of investment  funds.   It allocates resources among
states, sets national goals and guidelines, and supervises and assists the
states as necessary.  Each state has its own SWC and FAE which channel BNH
and state funds to individual projects, execute investments and operate
systems.   All funds flow through an appointed financial intermediary in
each state, generally the state Bank, which acts as agent for both the FAE
and BNH.   Project preparation and execution are supervised by technical
agencies (OTs), appointed by BNH in each state.   BNH maintains   regional
offices (SOSAN) which supervise local activities and a central staff which
plans and supervises on a national level (COSAN).   Through PLANASA, na-
tional guidelines have been set for project design, economic evaluation of
projects and procurement.   A national  tariff law has also been enacted
(para.  1.18).   PLANASA's  targets  and operational goals act as a common
frame of reference against which individual investments may be judged and
which guide BNH's programming.   Aside  from providing new financial re-
sources and helping sector institutions to become more efficient, PLANASA
also promotes financial cross-subsidization.   At the state level, the ap-
plication of the same tariff in most localities results in a subsidy from
larger, richer cities to smaller, poorer towns.   BNH's terms for invest-
ments in small communities are softer than those for larger towns.  At the
national level, the cross-subsidy is achieved through differential interest
rates that vary in relation to the revenue of the state. Detailed informa-
tion on PLANASA is in the Project File.
1.10          Each S4C prepares annually a global feasibiiity report (EVG)
which sets forth service level objectives, construction schedules and a fi-
nancing program for a five-year period.  On the basis of these studies and
each state's commitment to provide one-half of the financing for the in-
vestment program, BNH enters into PLANASA agreements with each state. Each
SWC selects subprojects which are included in the EVG for BNH considera-
tion.   The SWC prepares feasibility studies (RTPs), including an analysis
of alternatives, for approval by the OT. COSAN formally reviews only about
5% of the RTPs presented by the SWCs.  The SWC then prepares final designs
under BNH financing.  After a cycle of review and approval similar to the
process mentioned above, BNH signs a contract (CTN or CTE) with the SWC to
finance the works. Since 1981, BNH has been using guidelines for economic
analysis of subprojects and selection criteria for all subprojects financed
under Bank loans (para. 3.05).
1.11         Brazilian authorities have set new and ambitious  goals  for
PLANASA in the 1980s.   The primary goals are to provide 90% of the urban
population with water supply and 65% with sewerage by the end of the de-
cade.   In addition, previous secondary goals have been re-emphasized and
new ones set:  the investment program for small communities is to be revi-
talized; SWC management is to be improved; research and institutional deve-
lopment will be emphasized; and further economies of scale will be sought.
A new emphasis on environmental protection is planned; and priority will be



- 4 -
given to sewerage and pollution control projects in major metropolitan
areas.  For financial reasons, basic sanitation projects are to be done in
stages and unaccounted-for water is to be reduced. 3/ Based on population
projections (para. 1.01), PLANASA's 1990 goals imply that 103 million
people will have to be served with water supply and 74 million with sewer-
age. Thus, during the period 1981-1990, 44 million additional people will
have to receive water services and 46 million, sewerage services.  These
amounts are, respectively, 1.4 times and 2.6 times the incremental popula-
tion served during the 1970s.
Past and Projected Sector Investments
1.12      Past and required sector investments are presented in Table 1.1:
Table 1.1: PAST AND REQUIRED INVESTMENT (expressed in 1982 US$ million)

North             86      278          223            29       593       1,936
Northeast      1,387    1,433            3           514    3,132          508
Southeast     3,847    2,014           (48)        1,830    3,948          116

During the 1970s, water supply investment totaled about US$6.2 billion.
The cost per incremental person served was US$187.  Total sewerage invest-
ment was about US$2.5 billion, and the cost per incremental population
served was US$145.   To achieve the 1990 service level targets, estimated
investments (in 1982 prices) of US$4.9 billion for water supply and US$10.4
billion for sewerage would be required.   Unit cost per additional person
served with water supply is expected to decrease in the 1980s to US$115 due
to available production capacity.  Unit cost per additional person receiv-
ing sewerage services is expected to increase to US$230 as more systems re-
quiring treatment are built.
1.13        PLANASA annual investments are projected to remain almost con-
stant at a level of US$1.4 billion in 1982 prices. The PLANASA investment
allocated to sewerage works also remains constant during the period 1983-87
at US$450 million per year or 25% of PLANASA investment.   Average annual
1982-85 funding of the sewerage subsector (US$570 million) is below the
annual investment levels needed (over US$1.0 billion) to reach the 1990
sewerage targets. Funding of the water supply subsector (annual average of
US$950 million over 1982-85) is above the annual investment levels needed
(over US$480 million) to reach the 1990 water supply goals, which
3/ Minister Mario David Andreazza, As Novas Metas do PLANASA, September
1981.



could allow resources to be shifted to sewerage.  Flood control and drain-
age investment is projected to increase in constant 1982 prices from US$139
million in 1982 to US$210 million in 1985.  Based on preliminary BNH in-
vestment allocations by state, investment priorities are projected to shift
from the Southeast region to regions with lower service levels.  The per-
centage of PLANASA water supply and sewerage investment allocated to the
Southeast should drop from 59% in 1983 to 47% in 1985. However, a further
shift of funds to the lesser-served regions (40%-Southeast, 60%-other re-
gions during the 1980s) is required if 1990 regional service targets are to
be met. Detailed information on sector investment is in the Project File.
1.14      PLANASA's investments during 1968-81 were financed by BNH (42%),
the FAEs (40%), and grants and developers' contributions (18%).  In addi-
tion, BNH provided supplemental loans (FINEST 1 and 2) to assist the states
in meeting their contributions to the FAEs.  FAEs are funded by state con-
tributions and debt service payments on previous loans to the SWCs.   The
maximum state contribution is 5% of its revenues (mainly sales tax). When
the state contribution reaches 3% of its revenues, the state may obtain
FINEST loans to make up the shortfall.   Investment in small communities
(less than 5,000 inhabitants) is mostly financed by state government
grants.
1.15     PLANASA's projected investments for 1983-90 will continue to  have
the same sources of financing; however, with the attainment of the National
Tariff Law (NTL) objectives beginning in 1983, the SWCs should generate a
share of their investment needs (para. 4.09).  Debt service repayments on
past FAE loans to the SWCs would increase allowing the states to reduce
direct financing.
Institutional Development
1.16      PLANASA has also been expanded with a special program for instit-
utional development (PRODISAN).  Key institutional and human resource pro-
blems of the SWCs were identified and specific projects have been establi-
shed. Selected SWCs are developing technical assistance which would be ap-
plied in other SWCs (para. 2.12). Under PRODISAN, BNH plays a coordinating
role and provides financial and technical resources. PAHO and the Bank as-
sist PRODISAN.   During 1981-85, BNH expects to execute through PRODISAN
over 500 technical assistance projects in planning, technical and commer-
cial operations, and finance and administration at a cost of US$306
million.
Evaluation of the National Plan
1.17          The overall policy framework and investment programming of
PLANASA are satisfactory.  National policies, goals and guidelines estab-
lished through PLANASA provide a common reference for the evaluation of the
SWCs' investment programs and individual projects and assist in the alloca-
tion of BNH's investment resources.   Investment planning is decentralized
to the state level.   BNH revises and consolidates  the SWCs'  investment
plans to develop the overall PLANASA investment program in accordance with



- 6-
the availability of resources. The Federal Government limited sector in-
vestment in 1982 to control inflation.   Limits have to be relaxed in the
near future if 1990 PLANASA goals are to be met.  The timing and amount of
the PLANASA investment program appear feasible. The imbalance in the allo-
cation of resources to less developed regions is partially being addressed
in the 1982-85 investment program; however, imbalance in the allocation to
sewerage is not addressed (para. 1.13). BNH plans to correct the imbalance
after 1985.
National Tariff Law
1.18       Water supply and sewerage tariffs are governed by the National
Tariff Law and implementing regulations introduced in 1978.   The NTL was
designed with Bank participation.   The objectives of the Law are to:  (a)
relate tariffs to the cost of services; (b) make services affordable to the
low income population; and (c) ensure the financial viability of the SWCs.
Under the legislation, the SWCs' revenues should cover the sum of operating
costs, depreciation and debt service.   The maximum allowed rate of return
is 12% of operating investments.  Regulations provide for gradual compli-
ance with the NTL objectives with full compliance scheduled for 1983.
Nineteen of the SWCs should comply in 1983; seven SWCs will require addi-
tional time to comply (paras. 4.09 and 4.10).  Full attainment of the NTL
objectives would permit a high level of cost recovery in the sector in com-
parison with other countries. Grants and subsidies should be limited. The
small communities program and subprojects meeting least cost and poverty
criteria (para. 3.05) would continue to receive grant financing.   Capital
contributions to the SWCs by the states should be eliminated when the NTL
objectives are met by the SWCs.   Interest charges on foreign borrowings
would continue to be subsidized because of the social nature of the sector
and BNH's operations (para. 4.04).
1.19        BNH received authority in 1982 to set tariffs for the sector
within general guidelines established by the Government (5% annual increase
in tariffs in real terms in 1982 and 1983). Because of high inflation, the
government and BNH have agreed that tariff levels should be adjusted on a
quarterly basis.  Tariff adjustments are reviewed jointly with the Bank.
BNH's performance in the analysis of the SWCs' tariff proposals has im-
proved considerably and now includes measures to enhance revenues and re-
duce costs.   Assurances were obtained during Loan negotiations that the
Bank, the Government and BNH will continue to jointly review tariff levels
on a quarterly basis.
1.20       The SWGs' tariff structures include residential, commercial, in-
dustrial and public consumption categories. All tariff structures are pro-
gressive with maximum residential charges per m3 ranging from 1.6 to 4.7
times the minimum charge.  Most companies have uniform state-wide tariffs.
Average water tariffs are relatively high. With the exception of the SWCs
in Rio de Janeiro and the Federal District, water tariffs range from
US$0.15/m3 to US$0.32/m3.  Sewerage charges are generally set at 80% to
100% of the water bill, although in some SWCs the charge is 60% or less,
In general, the tariff structures are adequate; however, improvements are



- 7 -
being made (lower minimum consumption block, reduction in consumption
levels at which excess charges are assessed, increase in sewerage charge to
100% of water billed, development of rates for sewage collection and treat-
ment). A consultant, employed through PAHO, is now reviewing the adequacy
of the tariff structures.   A study on rates for sewage collection and
treatment services under Loan 1525-BR has been completed.   Agreement was
reached during Loan negotiations upon a plan to implement the recommenda-
tions of such study and new rates for sewage collection and treatment
services.
Experience under Previous Loans and Current Strategy
1.21        The Bank has made loans amounting to US$769 million for nine
water supply and sewerage projects in Brazil.   Basic data, including cur-
rent status of each operation, are summarized in Annex 1.  Four completed
operations, in the states of Sao Paulo and Minas Gerais, achieved the main
project objectives, despite substantial delays attributed to lack of ex-
perience, inadequate institutional capabilities of the SWCs, failure of the
states to provide timely counterpart financing, and inadequate project pre-
paration. Current operations are progressing well except for the Sao Paulo
Sewage Treatment Project (Loan 1525-BR) where major project components have
been halted due to inadequate funding from the State and political pro-
blems.   BNH and SABESP (the executing agency) have agreed to meet with
newly elected government officials to resolve the current political impasse
and funding problems and present alternative solutions in May 1983.  The
Bank will then decide on the reinitation of the affected components, or the
cancellation of the respective portion of the Loan proceeds.  Execution of
the Third Minas Gerais Project (Loan 1850-BR) should b-e completed by the
end of 1984, as estimated at appraisal.  The Southern States Project (Loan
1823-BR) is scheduled for completion at the end of 1984, two years later
than the appraisal estimate.   Problems causing delays have been overcome
and the disbursement pace has accelerated. The institutional capabilities
of the SWCs under the Northeast (Loan 1656-BR) and Multistate (Loan
1970-BR) Projects are weaker than those under the other Bank operations.
This situation, together with recent BNH investment limitations (para.
1.17) and election year grant financing of otherwise eligible subprojects
in some states, has resulted in slow project execution and loan disburse-
ments.   Project implementation problems have been substantially resolved
under Loan 1656-BR, but continue under 1970-BR.  A mid-term review of the
latter project is scheduled for August 1983. Despite these problems, pro-
gress on implementing the many hundreds of subprojects by the 14 SWCs has
been impressive, reflecting the competence and continuity of Brazilian
sector management. As of December 31, 1982, 70% of prior Bank loans under
execution had been committed and 32%, disbursed.
1.22      Bank participation accounted for about 10% of PLANASA investment
in 1971-82.   In addition,  the Bank has played an important role in the
strengthening of the sector, in particular: (a) the development of a uni-
form accounting system for the SWCs; (b) the preparation and enactment of
the NTL and the implementation of tariff increases on a quarterly basis;



- 8 -
(c) the decentralization of BNH's project preparation and supervision acti-
vities to regional offices and technical agents; (d) the standardization of
the SWCs' procedures and information systems; and (e) the adoption by BNH
of basic criteria for economic analysis of subprojects. The Bank has also
fostered the growing participation of BNH in the Bank's appraisal and su-
pervision activities including procurement.
1.23      The increased maturity of Brazilian institutions makes it appro-
priate to pursue broader goals through sector lending. The Bank would help
consolidate the actions and reforms initiated through its on-going projects
geared at developing sector institutions, policies and operational proce-
dures.  Contributions made under previous operations would be extended to
all states.  Eligibility criteria for the SWCs to participate in the pro-
ject will be introduced  (para.  3,04).   Project selection criteria have
been improved.   Further improvements will include guidelines to determine
appropriateness of low cost, non-conventional sanitation alternatives as a
means to increase access to sanitation (para. 3.06). A recently initiated
study focusing on the rural, solid waste and pollution control subsectors
would form the basis for policy discussions with the Government. The Bank
would also continue its review and dialogue on tariffs, the institutional
strengthening of BNH and the allocation of sector resources. To complement
the series of sector loans envisaged in support of PLANASA, the Bank would
consider project loans to assist the three largest SWCs (in Minas Gerais,
Rio de Janeiro and Sao Paulo) which must cope with particularly severe re-
quirements for water supply and sanitation arising from rapid industriali-
zation and urban growth. The proposed sector loan would finance the prepa-
ration of the first of such projects (para. 3.03 (d)).
II. THE BORROWER AND THE BENEFICIARIES
The Borrower - BNH
2.01       As in previous operations, BNH would be the Borrower under the
proposed loan,  BNH is a government-owned bank, established in 1964.  BNH
is administratively and financially independent and reports to the Ministry
of Interior. Its lending activities are concentrated in low-cost housing,
urban development and sanitation.
2.02      BNH is administered by a Board of Directors and an Executive Com-
mittee,  The Board is composed of the Executive Committee plus nine coun-
selors.  The Executive Committee is chaired by the President and includes
the seven directors of BNH's Operating Departments  (Annex 2).   Four of
BNH's directorates are in charge of different housing and urban development
programs (including sanitation), and the remaining are responsible for
planning, administration and finance, and commercial operations.  BNH-Bank
relations are channelled through a technical advisory unit attached to the
Presidency.   Responsibility for decisions on routine matters is delegated
to regional Credit Committees.   BNH's accounting is computerized and re-
liable.   Standard financial statements following generally accepted ac-
counting principles are produced in a timely manner (para. 2.15).  Finan-
cial planning is satisfactory. Four year budgets are revised annually and



- 9 -
submitted to the Federal Government for approval.   Beginning in 1982, the
Federal Government has placed limits on BNH's investments as part of its
program to reduce inflation (Chapter IV).
2.03       The Operations Division of the Sanitation Finance System (COSAN)
was created in 1967, and is responsible for implementing PLANASA.   COSAN
coordinates project implementation, acts as the technical and financial
controller for the SWCs and proposes regulations for the sector.   COSAN
studies and proposes to the BNH Board terms for BNH and FAE loans, and
establishes tariffs for the SWCs. COSAN has a director, an assistant di-
rector,  three  department  chiefs  and  57  staff  members,  mostly  profes-
sionals. Some of COSAN's activities have been decentralized since 1976 to
its 26 regional offices (SOSAN).  Regional office functions include moni-
toring loan agreements between BNH and the SWCs, approving disbursements,
up-dating project execution and disbursement schedules, monitoring the per-
formance of the FAEs and the OTs, and reviewing the SWC's operations. The
OTs, highly technical institutions under contract with BNH, carry out most
project supervision including the monitoring of the effective use of pro-
curement guidelines (para. 3.13).
2.04        COSAN has performed satisfactorily overall, but some areas need
to be strengthened.   The  Bank and BNH agreed under Loans  1823-BR and
1970-BR to carry out a technical assistance program to COSAN. The program
comprises two stages:  (a) identification of institutional weaknesses with
corresponding recommendations; and (b) implementation of the recommenda-
tions.  Work will cover the areas of information, project preparation and
planning, supervision of project execution, and operational control of the
SWCs.  BNH decided to use PAHO as its consultant for this program.  Start-
up was delayed until a contract between BNH and PAHO was signed in December
1981.   Since then, the program manager and the BNH counterpart have been
appointed and consultants for the institutional analysis of COSAN have been
hired.   Studies started in October 1982.  Consultants' recommendations on
BNH's monitoring of the operations of the SWCs were presented to BNH in
December 1982. Recommendations for the other areas should be produced dur-
ing the first quarter of 1983 in accordance with contractual dates. Agree-
ment was reached between the Bank and BNH during Loan negotiations on the
recommendations made under the technical assistance program for the moni-
toring of the SWCs by BNH, and on a schedule for implementing the recommen-
dations.  Also, BNH agreed to complete the technical assistance program by
September 30, 1984.   Assurances were also provided for the periodic ex-
change of views on the progress made under the technical assistance pro-
gram.  BNH agreed under the Multistate Project to make semi-annual field
supervisions of each project SWC, and to submit supervision reports to the
Bank. COSAN has not fully complied with the reporting requirements. COSAN
sends, however, specialized staff to the SWCs to concentrate on specific
issues and relies on regional offices (SOSAN) and technical agencies (OTs)
for supervision. The Bank and BNH have recently agreed on a new format for
progress reports and a supervision program for 1982-83.   Assurances were
obtained during Loan negotiations that BNH will provide efficient and
timely project supervision and timely submit semi-annual progress reports
to the Bank (para. 3.16).



- 10
The Beneficiaries - SWCs
2.05      The 26 SWCs would be the beneficiaries and executing agencies for
subprojects financed under the proposed Loan, provided they comply with
eligibility and selection criteria agreed upon during Loan negotiations
(paras. 3.04-3.06).   All SWCs are owned by their state governments.  Each
has a board of directors appointed by the state governor for a period of
four years. The president of the board of directors is the chief executive
officer.   Each of the other directors is the working head of one of the
operational departments.  The boards are, in most cases, composed of qua-
lified, experienced professionals. The SWCs are headquartered in the state
capitals and are responsible for services in the capital city and up to
hundreds of small- or medium-size cities. The SWCs included in the project
have signed PLANASA agreements, have ongoing projects financed by BNH/FAE
and have completed EVGs.
2.06       Although procedures and operations have been standardized under
the long-term assistance of PAHO, the quality of management and organiza-
tion among the SWCs is uneven.  Organizational structures in all SWCs have
departments for engineering, operations, administration, commercial, and
finances and accounting.   Organizational structures are considered appro-
priate. An institutional analysis of each SWC is summarized in Annex 3.
2.07      Compliance with subloan covenants by the 14 SWCs included in the
Bank's current projects has been fair.  Exceptions refer to delays in sub-
miting proposals for sewage collection and treatment tariffs (SABESP), sub-
mission of EVGs (all SWCs in 1981, as new a format was being introduced),
establishment of internal audit units (para. 2.14), and tariffs (five SWCs
in 1982 - para. 4.07). The Bank will continue to work closely with BNH and
the SWCs on compliance with Loan covenants.  BNH's progress reports to the
Bank will include a review of compliance with covenants (para. 3.16).
2.08       Techinical operations and maintenance of existing systems are ge-
nerally satisfactory. Many SWCs, however, have difficulties operating dis-
tant systems, which may be up to 2,000 km away from headquarters. Commer-
cial organization and procedures are adequate.  Service regulations are in
force and billings and collections are, for the most part, computerized.
In most SWCs meters are read monthly and most consumers are billed within
15 days of the reading.   Collections are made by banks and SWC offices.
Sales in accounts receivable range from 1.2 to 4.8 months; the average is
2.3 months.   Consumer files and billing and collection performance can be
further improved. To this end, a new integrated commercial system is being
tested for use by all SWCs (para. 2.12 (e)). Low levels of both production
and consumption metering (average 40%) result in unreliable assessments of
unaccounted-for water (between 10-60%), and make cross-subsidization
through tariff structures difficult.   The project provides for a substan-
tial increase of production and consumption metering through specific sub-
projects (para. 2.12 (f)).
2.09      Human resources administration needs to be improved.  The number
of employees per thousand connections in the SWCs varies from 3.3 to 15.1.



- 11 -
Personnel costs, on average, account for over 60% of the SWCs' operating
costs; personnel turnover in the SWCs ranges from 1% to 13%.  To address
these problems, the SWCs are implementing personnel management systems, as
covenanted under Loan 1970-BR, that define qualifications, functions, re-
sponsibilities, possibilities for advancement, and salaries. Training pro-
grams, including management development seminars, are being developed
(para. 2.12 (h)) with assistance from the Brazilian Association of Sanitary
Engineers.
2.10       The planning process is satisfactory.   The SWCs and the state
governments establish priorities for the extension of water supply and
sewerage services based on assesed needs in the communities.   Using these
assessments, the SWC prepares demand projections, service levels targets,
investment schedules and financial projections in a standarized EVG format
(para.  1.10).   The EVG format has been progressively improved.   All SWCs
completed the 1983 version by September 1982 on the basis of guidelines
prepared by the SWC of Parana and BNH.   The format is deemed to require
only minor changes.
2.11      Engineering studies, design and supervision of works are executed
by competent consultants. The SWC engineering departments, with the advice
and control of the OTs, review designs and monitor construction.  Projects
are usually implemented on schedule, and project programming and control
procedures are now being strengthened.
2.12       PRODISAN is administered by COSAN and partially financed by the
Bank.   PRODISAN has assisted the SWCs in identifying their institutional
weaknesses and preparing their own technical lssistan .e programs.  In addi-
tion, PRODISAN has recently drawn on the expertise of the SWCs to develop
pilot technical assistance projects to address common constraints identi-
fied in the areas of (a) EVG, (b) program budget, (c) commercial opera-
tions, (d) consumer files, (e) billing and collection, (f) unaccounted-for
water, (g) management of fixed assets, and (h) human resources administra-
tion and training.  The experience will, in turn, be transferred gradually
to all SWCs.   Because of the difficulties  involved in the execution of
these pilot projects and the different degree of managerial capacity in the
SWCs, BNH has decided to concentrate its efforts on a selected number of
SWCs and projects.   This approach was discussed with BNH during appraisal
and found practical.   During Loan negotiations agreement was obtained on
the pilot technical assistance projects which should be given priority and
on a schedule for implementing these projects in the SWCs. Assurances were
also obtained that on-lending agreements with the SWCs will provide for
implementation timetables for their technical assistance programs.
2A13       Al  SWCs use the ur_fo-. accounting system introduced by BNH in
1976.  The system is satisfactory, although its reliability needs to be im-
proved.  Results are usually available within two months after closing of
the accounting period  (calendar year reporting).   Traditional financial
statements are produced routinely. Cost accounting is at an early stage of
implementation, but large SWCs have included the establishment of cost ac-
counting systems in their institutional development programs.   Financial



- 12 -
control systems are adequate with the SWCs preparing and using annual bud-
gets to control expenditures.  Program budgeting is to be introduced  in
the SWCs (para.  2.12 (b)).   All SWCs have computerized their accounting
systems.   The quality of data processing services  is fair,  but varies
widely among the SWCs.   Generally,  the states require the SWCs to use
government-run service bureaus for at least a portion of their computing
needs. Computing activities are the most significant category of the SWCs'
operating costs after personnel and energy. BNH is now completing a survey
of existing hardware, applications and costs in order to achieve the pro-
gressive standardization of equipment, improve performance and reduce
costs.
2.14       About one-half of the SWCs have internal audit departments.  The
complexity of the SWCs requires the prompt establishment of internal audit
offices in all SWCs. Establishment of internal audit units in ten SWCs was
covenanted under previous Bank loans.  At year-end 1982, audit departments
had been created in five of these SWCs.  Assurances were obtained from BNH
that internal audit offices will be established in all project SWCs by
December 31, 1984, staffed at levels satisfactory to the Bank and BNH, and
maintained thereafter.
Audit
2.15       The SWCs retain qualified external auditors who must be regis-
tered with BNH. External auditors qualified or disclaimed opinions on the
1981 financial statements of nine SWCs because of problems with accounts
receivable and/or fixed assets accounting. The project would support meas-
ures to address these deficiencies (para. 2.12 (c)-(g)).   BNH's external
auditor is Mario Rocha Auditores Asociados, a large, respected firm with
offices in all major Brazilian cities. The firm issued an unqualified opi-
nion on BNH's 1981 financial statements. Assurances were obtained from BNH
during negotiations that:   (a) accounts and financial statements for BNH
and the SWCs for each fiscal year, starting with fiscal year 1983, will be
audited by independent auditors, satisfactory to the Bank, and in accord-
ance with generally accepted auditing standards; and (b) certified copies
of the audited financial statements and audit report together with a state-
ment on compliance with the Bank's financial covenants will be submitted to
the Bank by April 30 for BNH and May 31 for the SWCs of each subsequent
year. BNH's external auditor should also express an opinion on statements
of expenditures used to support claims for disbursements and the adequacy
of associated internal control (para. 3.22).
III. THE PROJECT
Background
3.01       The sector loan follows up previous Bank operations which began
in 1971.   These operations have permitted the Bank to gradually move to
sector lending.  The Bank has nurtured sector financial viability and the
institutional capability of BNH and the SWCs to plan and execute PLANASA



- 13 -
investments (para.  1.22).   The project,  to be executed in 1983-1987, is
designed to meet priority needs for water supply, sewerage and pollution
control in Brazil's urban areas. Four Bank missions assisted BNH with pro-
ject identification and preparation since the Government requested Bank
participation in mid-1981.
Project Objectives
3.02      The main project objectives are to:
(a)   improve  environmental  and  health  conditions  in  urban  areas
through the extension of water and sewerage services mostly to
low income areas, and the provision of sewage treatment where
pollution problems are more pressing;
(b)   support economic growth with a wide distribution of benefits by
providing adequate infrastructure, emphasizing the use of low
cost technology;
(c)   promote the efficient utilization of investment resources within
the sector through improving the capacity of BNH and the SWCs to
plan, appraise and execute projects and through the achievement
of sector financial viability; and
(d)   help optimize the use of existing infrastructure and human re-
sources through institutional development and training.
Project Concept
3.03      The proposed project, represents about 60% of the new PLANASA in-
vestment commitments for 1983-84 or about 14% of total planned new invest-
ment during the implementation period 1983-87.  It would consist of: (a) a
group of water supply and sewerage subprojects meeting agreed upon selec-
tion criteria (para. 3.06); (b) engineering and consulting services as re-
quired for design and construction supervision; (c) institutional develop-
ment programs for the SWCs,4/ including consulting services and equipment;
and (d) consulting services for the preparation of a sanitation project for
the metropolitan area of Rio de Janeiro.   Almost 80% of the Rio project
cost would be for low cost sewerage systems in low income neighborhoods,
which are facing critical sanitary conditions, affecting over four million
people.
3.04      Subprojects from all SWCs would, in principle, be accepted in the
proposed project.   Before being eligible for subproject financing (para.
3.12), the SWCs would have to comply with the following conditions: (a)
presentation of an EVG 1983-87 (para. 1.10), satisfactory to BNH and the
Bank; (b) presentation of an institutional development program, satisfac-
tory to BNH and the Bank (para.1.16); (c) compliance with the Tariff Law,
4/ The project does not provide funding for institutional development of
BNH. Funding for this program is included under Loans 1823-BR and
1970-BR (para. 2.04).



14
or in special cases, presentation of a plan, satisfactory to BNH and the
Bank, to achieve compliance in 1985 at the latest (para. 4.10); (d) compli-
ance with existing loan covenants if already included in current Bank pro-
jects; and (e) full commitment of loan funds if already included in current
Bank projects. During Loan negotiations, assurances were obtained from the
Borrower that (a) the SWCs will meet the above eligibility criteria prior
to becoming Loan beneficiaries and (b) BNH will annually review compliance
by each SWC, and prepare proposals satisfactory to the Bank for compliance
or suspension of new financing of subprojects, if necessary.   Because of
the substantial investment programs of the SWCs in Sao Paulo, Rio de
Janeiro and Minas Gerais, and the need to maintain a balanced sector ap-
proach, BNH also provided assurances   during negotiations that Bank dis-
bursements for subprojects in the three states combined would be limited to
45% of the proposed loan amount.
Selection Criteria
3.05      Analysis of subprojects under PLANASA is based on simple criteria
agreed upon under previous Bank financed projects and applicable to all
subprojects financed by BNH loans to the SWCs.  The criteria include:  (a)
selection of the least cost alternative; (b) cut-off criteria based on per
capita investment cost for villages   and small communities;  (c) average
long-term incremental cost (marginal cost) for medium and large cities. In
cities with 50,000 or more inhabitants, the present tariff or the cove-
nanted tariff in the next twelve months should exceed 75% of the marginal
cost of such subprojects (equivalent on average to an internal financial
rate of return of 5% or more). For water supply subprojects in cities with
5,000 to 50,000 inhabitants and all sewerage subprojects, the applicable
tariff (water or sewerage) should be at least 60% of the marginal cost of
each subproject  (equivalent  to a return of 1% or more).   Projects not
satisfying the above conditions but which are redesigned for minimum cost
may be accepted by the Bank on a case by case basis.   The analysis consi-
ders (a) poverty levels and health conditions; (b) the lack of alternative
water sources; (c) the impossibility of recovering costs through tariffs;
and (d) an evaluation of benefits in other sectors of the economy.  These
projects are likely to be in towns where urban poverty prevails and large
investments are needed to compensate for the lack of previous investments.
The Bank participates in the financing of grant financed subprojects when
the poverty level is higher than 70%.
3.06       BNH has applied the current selection criteria to some two hun-
dred subprojects during the last year.  The results reveal the need for
some improvements,  The least cost analysis for sewerage usually does not
include alternatives for the improvement of existing individual systems or
construction of non-conventional, low cost technology systems.  The use of
per capita cost as a cut-off criteria for each small community does not fit
well with BNH's practice of dealing with packages of small communities.
Many subprojects, particularly those in medium cities, which achieve ac-
ceptable internal financial rates of return have not met the marginal cost
cut-off criteria.  The current criteria are being modified by: (a) includ-
ing a check list of items for least-cost analysis of sewerage subprojects;



- 15 -
(b) applying average investment per capita costs to groups of subprojects
for small communities; and (c) using rate of return cut-off criteria for
subprojects that do not meet the marginal cost criteria.  The cut-off rates
of return would be the same as the average equivalent returns under exist-
ing selection criteria.   In cases where the rate of return is used, BNH
should demonstrate that the subproject would not have a negative effect on
the SWC's cash flow. Agreement was reached during Loan negotiations on the
proposed selection criteria (Annex 5), and assurances were obtained from
BNH that the criteria will be applied to all subprojects to be financed
under the Loan.
Service Standards
3.07      Service standards under the project will consist of water supply
by house connections and sewage disposal by public systems in most cities.
Standards are in accordance with proper management practices and the hab-
its, expectations and ability to pay of the populations benefitted. Conso-
lidated service levels for each SWC for the project implementation period
have been prepared and are presented in Annex 14.  Works are appropriately
staged to suit the least-cost solutions.  Existing facilities in most pro-
ject cities will be upgraded and/or expanded in order to extend their use-
ful lives.  Distribution networks will be extended to new areas.  These ex-
tensions are being designed to conform with accepted BNH standards. Empha-
sis is given in the project to the use of low-cost sewage disposal techni-
ques (para. 3.02 (b)).
Project Cost
3.08         fhe total cost of the project is estimated at US$863 million
equivalent (Table 3.1).   The foreign exchange component is estimated at
US$263 million (30.6% of total).  The local component includes about US$30
million equivalent of indirect taxes on industrial products.   Base costs
are expressed in prices of Julv 1982.  The project cost includes: (a) 10%
of the construction cost for engineering expense related to the project;
(b) 10% of the base cost for physical contingencies; and (c) 27.6% of the
July 1982 project cost for price escalation  (US$186.9 million).   Due to
high internal inflation rates, project cost estimates were prepared in UPCs
(commonly used Brazilian monetary unit equal to the value of indexed na-
tional treasury bonds).   Since 1981 the value of the UPC in equivalent US
dollars has been maintained (1 UPC=US$10.8). The value of the UPC in equi-
valent US dollars is expected to be maintained over the project disburse-
ment period. Price contingencies for foreign and local cost (in UPCs) were
calculated using the following rates of inflation:   1982, 8%;  1983, 8%;
1984, '5%. 1985, 7%; and 1986 and thereafter, 6%.
3.09      The total cost of the project was determined on the basis of the
1983-87 investment programs of the 26 SWCs, as defined in the EVGs.   Dur-
ing Loan negotiations, assurances were obtained from BNH that the Bank will
have the opportunity to comment on major changes in the EVGs.  Project cost
would represent about 60% of new investments in 1983-84, after allowing for
full commitment of Bank financed projects under implementation and the



- 16 -
limitations on investment in Sao Paulo, Rio de Janeiro and Minas Gerais.
Detailed investments by company are shown in Annex 6.

3. 10     Cost estimates for major subprojects are based on bills of quan-
tities and unit prices prepared by consultants, the SWCs and BNH.  Estim-
ates for other subprojects are based on unit costs for similar works.
About 1,280 man-months of consulting services are included for the tech-
nical assistance to SWCs.  About 90 man-months of consultant services are
included for the preparation of the Rio de Janeiro Sanitation Project.
Completion of final engineering designs and construction supervision will
require about  2,790 man-months  of consulting services.   Total estimated
cost of consulting and engineering services is US$27.6 million.  The aver-
age cost per man-month (US$6,640), represents the man-month rate as billed
by consulting firms for individuals and includes salaries, social costs,
and the firms' overhead and profits.   Because of the technical ability of
local consulting firms (often working in joint ventures with foreign
firms), no direct expatriate consultants' input is expected except for
highly technical studies for the preparation of the Rio de Janeiro Project.
Project Financing
3.11       The SWCs' investment programs are to be financed by loans from
BNH and the FAEs (para. 1.14).  The Bank Loan of US$302.3 million (35% of
the total project cost) would cover the estimated foreign exchange cost of



- 17 -
the project of US$263 million, US$37 million of domestic expenditures and
US$2.3 million of the front-end fee. Financing of local costs is justified
on the basis of achieving a reasonable participation by the Bank in total
project costs.  The balance would be financed by BNH funds (15%) and loans
from the FAEs (50%). BNH intends to obtain co-financing of US$100 million.
Project Implementation
3.12       The Loan would be made to BNH with the guarantee of the Govern-
ment.  The 26 SWCs would be eligible as sub-borrowers and beneficiaries.  A
project loan agreement (para. 1.10) signed by BNH and each State and the
SWC will be used for each subproject to be financed.   This agreement will
include  general lending conditions and commitments  detailed in Annex 7.
The Bank obtained BNH agreement during Loan negotiations on: (a) the terms
and conditions of the project loan agreements; (b) the signature by at
least three States and SWCs of their first project loan agreement as a con-
dition of Loan effectiveness; and (c) the signature by the State and SWC of
the project loan agreement with BNH (CTN or CTE) as a condition for disbur-
sements of loan proceeds for the subproject.
3.13       The executing agencies for the proposed project will be the SWCs
for investments in the project states, and BNH for preparation of the Rio
de Janeiro Sanitation Project.   The individual subprojects are being pre-
pared and would be supervised by qualified consultants retained by the SWCs
and by the OTs. BNH has responsibility for coordinating the entire project
and providing technical assistance to the SWCs.
3.14       The project will be implemented during 1983-87.  Annual project
investments are presented in Annex 8.  These investment tables, which have
been agreed upon with BNH, will form the basis for progress reporting.
This investment schedule (Annex 8) is compatible with the executing capabi-
lities of each SWC.  The project is expected to be completed by December
31, 1987.  To avoid delays assurances were obtained from BNH that all Bank
Loan funds will be committed through on-lending agreements (CTN or CTE) by
December 31, 1986.
Status of Project Preparation
3.15          Project preparation conforms  to the proposed  implementation
schedule.   Final designs for major works which are expected to start in
1983 are being completed by consultants.   Designs for extensions of water
distribution or sewage systems in many cases follow existing master plans
and only minor revisions are needed.   Designs for medium-size and small
communities, in most cases, require a lead time of only two to three
months.   In order to assure an early start on disbursement of Loan funds
after signature, BNH has approved final designs corresponding to 15% of the
total estimated project cost.  These designs are now being reviewed by the
Bank.



- 18 -
Project Supervision
3.16       Project supervision by BNH should allow the Bank to sustain pre-
sent sector lending levels with reasonable Bank manpower input.  Bank su-
pervision would concentrate on the strengthening of the PLANASA system and
sector-wide issues.   BNH's general supervision activities include annual
reviews of EVGs and institutional development programs, semi-annual reviews
of tariff proposals, and review of engineering and economic studies of
major subprojects. Direct project supervision would continue to be carried
out by the SWCs own staff or private consultants with assistance from SOSAN
and the OTs.   Assurances were obtained from BNH during Loan negotiations
that (a) its staff will make semi-annual field supervisions of each project
SWC; (b) will submit to the Bank by April 30 and October 31 of each year a
satisfactory report on the status of project implementation, including com-
pliance with Loan covenants, technical assistance for COSAN and monitoring
indicators (para. 4.12); and (c) submit to the Bank a Project Completion
report within four months of Loan closing.
3.17       The Bank will review and approve all subprojects in cities with
estimated investments of US$8 million or more.  The aggregate of these sub-
projects would represent about 80% of the programmed investments but
only about 60 subprojects.   Subprojects in cities in which the estimated
investment is less than US$8 million would be approved by BNH subject to
ex post Bank review. This arrangement, now in operation for all water and
sewerage loans in Brazil, is working satisfactorily and is consistent with
BNH's present supervision capabilities.
Procurement
3.18      Purchases of goods and services will be processed under contracts
awarded in accordance with Bank guidelines, on the basis of international
competitive bidding.   BNH uses detailed guidelines, consistent with those
of the Bank (Annex 9). Contracts for equipment and materials estimated to
cost less than the equivalent of US$500,000 and contracts for civil works
estimated to cost less than the equivalent of US$3.0 million may be pro-
cured in accordance with locally advertised bidding procedures which are
satisfactory to the Bank.   In evaluation of bids for equipment and mate-
rials, local bidders will be allowed a margin of preference of either 15%
of the CIF price or the applicable import duty, whichever is lower. Appro-
ximately 60% of the goods and services should be procured using interna-
tional competitive bidding.  A general procurement notice was published in
the Development Forum. Assurances were obtained from BNH during Loan nego-
tiations that agreed upon procurement guidelines will be used in all procu-
rement processes under the project and that the general procurement notice
will be up-dated annually.
3.19       The Bank delegated the ex ante review of procurement decisions
to BNH under previous loans.  These procedures, which are detailed in  An-
nex 9, have resulted in faster decisions and project execution.  The Bank
would carry-out ex post review by sampling, and if it is determined that



- 19 -
procurement is inconsistent with agreed procedures, no expenditures for
such items would be financed out of the proceeds of the Loan and the Bank
may cancel such amount of the Loan.   Since the approval of Loan 1970-BR,
the Bank's ex post reviews have confirmed the adequacy of procurement
procedures.
Disbursement
3.20      The loan will be disbursed as follows:  (a)  68% of BNH's disbur-
sements to SWCs for eligible subprojects or technical assistance programs;
(b) 34% of BNH's disbursements to the states under the FINEST II line of
credit for approved subprojects; (c) 68% of BNH's disbursements to the
states under the FINEST III line of credit for programs in small communi-
ties; (d) 34% of Federal or State grants allocated to the SWCs for water or
sewerage subprojects in which at least 70% of the population benefitted are
urban poor; and (e) 100% of foreign expenditures and 50% of local expendi-
tures for consulting services for project preparation of the Rio de Janeiro
Sanitation Project.   For disbursements under  (a) through (d) above,  the
Bank would finance 34% of the cost of subprojects excluding financial and
technical charges.  Differences in disbursement percentages are due to BNH
financing 100% of the costs of FINEST II and grants and 50% of the costs of
the other lines (Annex 4).
3.21       BNH and the Bank agreed during Loan Negotiations on the need of
providing retroactive financing of up to US$15 million for project expendi-
tures incurred after January 1, 1983.  Such expenditures would be financed
if the following conditions are met:  (i) the SWC and the subproject compi-
with the eligibility and selection criteria, respectuvely; (ii) the CTN is
amended to include the terms and conditions of the Loan; and (iii) Bank
procurement guidelines have been followed.
3.22      A Special Account, in the amount of US$20 million, will be estab-
lished in the Banco Central do Brasil. This procedure reduces the time lag
between BNH's disbursements and Bank reimbursements to BNH.  The time lag
results in an erosion of the exchange rate at which BNH is reimbursed by
the Bank.   A similar account was established under Loan 1970-BR and is
working satisfactorily.   The advances would be used to finance only the
Bank's share of the project cost.   BNH would be entitled to make with-
drawals from the Special Account at the exchange rate applicable on the day
BNH disbursed its funds.  Loan disbursements (with the exception of with-
drawals incurred for consulting services for preparation of the Rio de
Janeiro Sanitation Project, which will be fully documented) will be made
against qtatements of expenditures prepared by BNH for each subproject, a
procedure tha. has worked successfully under previous Bank loans.  The de-
tailed docum_atation will be reLained by the beneficiary for inspection
during supervision missions and for audit by external auditors.   No force
account works  are envisaged.   Disbursement  procedures are explained in
Annex 10.  The estimated schedule of disbursements (Annex 11) assumes that
the Loan will be disbursed in six years compared to the average disburse-
ment period for past water supply loans in the Latin American Region of



- 20 -
eight years.  The more rapid disbursement is expected to occur because of
favorable prior Bank experience with BNH, particularly the three multi-
state projects,  and the three year commitment period.   The Closing Date
will be September 30, 1989.
Environmental Aspects
3.23       The project will improve environmental conditions in subproject
cities by extending basic sanitation services. Water supplies will be con-
structed or expanded to reach mainly low income areas of the cities or
small communities.   Sewage disposal facilities will be constructed to deal
with increased volumes of sewage resulting from the expansion of water sup-
plies.   The increase  in volume of sewage discharged in water courses in
some of the large and medium-size cities will require the construction of
treatment or special disposal facilities. The project includes the financ-
ing of studies for a pollution control project in the metropolitan area of
Rio de Janeiro as well as studies on low cost technologies for domestic
sewage disposal.  The monitoring and control of water quality would be en-
hanced by the project through the provision of laboratory equipment.
IV. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
The Borrower - BNH
Past and Present Finances
4.01        BNH's principal sources of funds are deposits in the Employee
Indemnity Fund (FGTS).  The FGTS is financed by an 8% payroll contribution
from all private sector employees covered by labor legislation.   Partici-
pants in the FGTS may withdraw their contributions plus interest when un-
employed or for house purchase or retirement.
4.02       Summary BNH financial statements are presented in Table 4.1 (de-
tails in Annex 12).  BNH continued to expand its operations during 1980-81,
although at lower growth rates than those achieved in the 1970s.  The loan
portfolio increased in 1980-81 at an average annual rate of 23%, while
total assets increased 17% p.a. Net receipts of the FGTS also increased by
17% annually.   During 1980-81, operating receipts increased 13% p.a. as a
result of slightly lower portfolio yields (para. 4.04) while operating ex-
penses were up 31% p.a. due to the lagging of the UPC (para. 4.03) and
higher administrative costs resulting from BNH's decentralization of opera-
tions. Internal cash generation dropped from UPC 42 million in 1979 to UPC
32 million in 1981.5/
4.03         BNH's total assets at December 31, 1981, were UPC 1,507 mil-
lion. The loan portfolio amounted to UPC 1,386 million or 92% of total as-
sets at December 31, 1981. Loans are backed by state and municipal govern-
ment and financial institution guarantees and mortgages. The allowance for
5/ 1 UPC = 10.8 US$ as of July 1982.



- 21 -
doubtful loans is legally set at one percent of loans outstanding. A loans
receivable aging at December 31, 1981, showed two percent of the loan port-
folio past due. BNH's equity (UPC 132 million) was a reasonable 10% of its
loan portfolio at December 31, 1981.  The FGTS amounted to UPC 1,137 mil-
lion, or 75% of BNH's total liabilities at December 31, 1981.  Other ele-
ments of BNH's capital structure at December 31, 1981, included housing and
savings and loan systems deposits (UPC 103 million: 7% of liabilities and
equity) and outstanding loans payable (UPC 37 million: 2% of liabilities
and equity). BNH's financial position is relatively well protected against
inflation as its loan portfolio and liabilities are denominated in UPCs and
excess cash is invested in government securities (ORTN) that are indexed
with inflation.  Adjustments to the UPC of 41% in 1979 and 55% in 1980
lagged considerably below the inflation rate of 77% recorded in 1979 and
91% in 1980.  This resulted in a transfer of resources from FGTS partici-
pants  to beneficiaries  of BNH loans.   Beginning in 1981,  however,  the
Government has maintained a policy of adjusting the UPC with inflation.
4.04       BNH lending increased from UPC 218 million in 1979 to UPC 325
million in 1981, an annual average increase of 22%. Lending to the sanita-
tion sector during this period was up 58% p.a. and totalled UPC 96 million
in 1981.  BNH annually prepares and up-dates a four-year lending program.
Sectoral and regional loan allocations are determined by the government's
public investment policy; sector demand and absorptive capacity; undis-
bursed funds under existing contracts; limits on indebtedness; and BNH's
profitability. Total lending in 1982 decreased slightly to UPC 321 million
as a result of the government's policy to control inflation by limiting
public investment. Lending for sanitation in 1982 is estimated at UPC 89
million, 7% lower than in 1981. BNH policies have increasingly emphasized
social interest loans addressed to poorer regions and lower ranges of the
housing market, resulting in a slight decrease in loan portfolio yields
(5.4% in 1979 versus 5.2% in 1981).   BNH interest rates for housing in-
crease with the size of the loan and range from 1% to 9%. Interest rates
for sanitation and urban development increase with, inter alia, the income
of the state and range from 1% to 10.5% (Annex 4). Terms for sanitation
loans are 18 years for water supply projects including an 18 month grace
period and 30 years for sewerage projects including a 54 month grace
period.   To improve its profitability, BNH increased interest charges on
sanitation and urban development loans in May 1982 and plans to increase
its loan administration fees in 1983.   BNH annual financial expense on
average liabilities and equity dropped slightly from 3.12% in 1979 to 3.10%
in 1981.  BNH pays 3.5% p.a. on the FGTS.  The Government subsidizes BNH's
cost of external debt with official development institutions by assuming
losses on the (a) spread between the interest charges on such debt and
BNH's on-lending rates and (b) foreign exchange after monetary correction.
The Government policy, which is justified in view of the social nature of
BNH's operations and the high level of cost recovery involved (para. 1.18)
would be continued for the proposed Bank loan and extended to commercial
bank co-financing included in the proposed project.



- 22 -
Future Finances
4.05       BNH's sources and applications of funds for 1983-1987 are sum-
marized in Table 4.1 (details in Annex 12).  For the period under review,
FGTS deposits are expected to grow 7% p.a. and should amount to UPC 960
million, or 42% of BNH's total sources of funds.   Loan repayments should
contribute UPC 620 million, or 27% of BNH's funds.  Internal cash genera-
tion is expected to improve over the five years, averaging UPC 52 million
and providing 11% of BNH's funds.  External borrowings from the Bank and
commercial bank co-financing should provide UPC 100 million, or 4% of BNH's
funds.   New lending activity is projected to grow at 5% p.a., totalling
about UPC 1,900 million during 1983-87.   BNH sanitation lending is pro-
jected at UPC 460 million over 1983-87, or 25% of total BNH lending.
Government limits on BNH lending are expected to be continued in 1983 with
a relaxation beginning in 1984. BNH's financial projections do not include
possible additional funding from the recently created FINSOCIAL (housing,
education and nutrition fund financed by a revenue tax and administered by
the National Development Bank) as the amounts to be allocated and terms and
conditions  of these  funds are still being discussed,   BNH's  financial
performance could be affected by the uncertainties associated with the
Brazilian economic outlook over the next few years. The uncertainties will
be alleviated to the extent that the Government's adjustment efforts
restore the conditions for sustained growth.
The Beneficiaries - SWCs
4.06       Two principal factors have to be considered when analyzing the
SWCs' financial performance:   their sources of funds to finance their in-
vestment programs and the objective of the NTL to ensure their financial
viability (para. 1.18).   The SWCs' investment programs have been almost
100% financed by BNX and FAE loans and government and developers' grants,
lessening the need for substantial internal cash generation.   Compliance
with the NTL in 1983 and thereafter in well managed SWCs should result in
full debt service and working capital coverage, positive internal contribu-
tion to investment and satisfactory rates of return.  Also, as their reve-
nues grow, the SWCs should be able to reduce their dependence on BNH and
FAE loans, freeing funds for other activities, including rural sanitation,
pollution control and solid waste management.  The Bank's revenue covenant
for the 14 SWCs under existing loans allows for gradual compliance with the
NTL in 1981 and 1982 with full compliance in 1983 and thereafter.  The re-
venue covenant for the proposed project will require full compliance with
the NTL.
1981-82 Financial Performance and Current Position
4.07       Summary financial data for the SWCs are presented in Annex 13
(details in the Project File).  Financial performance of the 14 SWCs under
on-going Bank loans has improved.  Five SWCs complied with the Bank's re-
venue covenant in 1981 and nine complied in 1982, based on estimated re-
sults. Of the 12 new companies to be considered in the proposed project,
two SWCs complied with the stricter requirements of the NTL in 1981 and
two complied  in 1982.   The improvement  resulted from tariff  increases

in real terms granted in 1981-82 for almost all companies combined with a
program of cost reductions and debt service assumptions by the states.  The
number of SWCs covering their working capital and debt service requirements
increased from ten in 1981 to 18 in 1982.  Companies with positive rates of
return increased from 15 in 1981 to 18 in 1982.  Debt/equity ratios at the
end of 1981 ranged from a high of 3.5:1 to SWCs with no debt servicing re-
quirements.   Several of the companies have been affected by delays in ac-
counts receivable collections which represent losses in real terms in high
inflationary periods.   Fixed assets are understated due to incomplete in-
ventories and revaluation based on an ORTN index which historically through
1980 was less than inflation (para. 4.03). Also in some SWCs the composite
depreciation rate is less than 2%, which is insufficient to spread the cost
of fixed assets over their estimated useful lives.   The project includes
measures to address these deficiencies (para. 2.12).
Future Finances
4.08        A table in Annex 13 summarizes for each SWC the year in which
specific financial targets should be met during the project implementation
period.   These  targets  include  compliance  with  the NTL,  positive  cash
flows, rates of return and the debt service ratio.   The SWCs' financial
projections are expressed in UPCs which are expected to maintain their
value against the US dollar in accordance with Government policy.  Operat-
ing improvements are assumed to be achieved, principally in unaccounted-for
water, employees/thousand connections and accounts receivable collections.
Inflation rates assumed for projection purposes are 8%-1983; 7.5%-1984;
7%-1985, and 6% p.a. thereafter.   Summary financial data for each of the
SWCs are presented in Annex 13 (details in the Project File).
4.09      Nineteen companies should comply with the NTL in 1983, including
12 of the  14 SWCs currently receiving Bank financing.   These companies
should all meet their working capital needs and achieve debt service ratios
of greater than 1.3 and positive contribution to investment.   The debt/
equity ratio should not exceed 3:1 for any SWC in this group.   Projected
rates of return should range from 1% to 9%.  Projected tariff increases to
allow compliance in 1984 and thereafter are in line with or less than pre-
sent Government guidelines for public utilities.
4.10        The seven SWCs not expected to comply with the NTL in 1983 are
relatively small. Using volume of water sold as a measure, these SWCs ac-
count for only 4% of sector activity.  They would require tariff increases
ranging from 10% to 47% in real terms to gain compliance.  These increases
are well the above Government's guidelines for 1983 public service tariff
adjustments (maximum 5% increase in real terms). These guidelines are con-
sistent with overall goals to control inflation, increase operating effi-
ciencies and reduce costs in public service institutions. The Bank and BNH
agreed on plans for compliance with the NTL by 1985 for the SWCs of Paraiba
(CAGEPA), Mato Grosso (SANEMAT), Mato Grosso do Sul (SANESUL) and Maranhao
(CAEMA). These plans include specific annual financial targets. The three
smallest SWCs of Acre (SANACRE), Amapa (CAESA) and Roraima (CAER) are not
expected to comply by 1985 and, in consequence, will not benefit from the
project (Annex 13).   Assurances from the Federal Government and BNH were



- 25 -
obtained that the revenue covenant and financial recovery plans will be met
in each year.   Assurances were also obtained during negotiations that BNH
would annually review compliance with the revenue covenant and recovery
plans and propose remedial action including suspension of new financing
when necessary.   Overall sector financial performance   should be satis-
factory with 23 SWCs, accounting for 99% of volume sold, attaining the NTL
objectives in 1985.
Financing Plan
4.11        The sector financing plan (1983-87) is presented in Table 4.2.
Total sector investment is estimated at UPC 884 million (US$9.5 bil-
lion) over the five year period. BNH and the FAEs would finance UPC 537.2
million (60%) of the investment program including engineering and adminis-
trative expenses and interest during construction.  Government and develo-
pers' contributions would finance UPC 160.2 million (18%) and the SWCs
would cover UPC 113 million (13%) of capital expenditures.  In addition to
direct loans of UPC   229.3 million to the SWCs, BNH would provide the
states  with UPC  49.7 million  in supplemental  loans  to the FAEs.   The
states' net funding of the investment program through the FAEs, grants and
debt service payments on previous BNH supplemental loans would amount to
3.3% of the states tax revenues, which is below the maximum 5% contrib-
ution.   Bank Loans,  including the proposed operation, would finance UPC
64.4 million of sector investment. Co-financing of US$100 million (UPC 9.2
million) would be sought to assist BNH in meeting its financing require-
ments. During Loan negotiations assurances were obtained from the Federal
Government and BNH on the timely availability of counterpart funds. Agree-
ment was also reached on the states' obligations to provide the required
counterpart funds as part of the general on-lending conditions and that by
November of each year counterpart fund requirements will be reviewed.

V. ECONOMIC AND SOCIAL ANALYSIS
Introduction
5.01        The project supports Government efforts to achieve a more bal-
anced development in the sector by: (a) providing water and sewerage serv-
ices to deprived regions; and (b) improving existing water supply and
sewerage services.   The project will benefit fifteen million urban poor
(paras. 5.06 and 5.07). The project emphasizes investments in medium-sized
cities (pop. 5,000-50,000) where the incidence of poverty is high and large
investments are needed to compensate for the lack of previous investments.
It will also make water supply services available to many small communi-
ties. The project would also continue to contribute to the strengthening
of BNH and the SWCs capacity to plan, appraise and execute projects,
thereby promoting the efficient utilization of investment resources within
the sector.  One important aspect of this is the use of BNH guidelines for
least-cost and economic analysis of subprojects.
5.02       As in all water supply and sewerage projects, the full economic
and social impact of this project cannot be evaluated through a simple rate
of return. Some well-known benefits, such as improvement of public health,
increases in effective nutrition due to reduced internal parasites, greater
well-being of the population, effects of progressive tariffs on the distri-
bution of income and services, and enhanced property values cannot be quan-
tified reliably and are therefore excluded from the rate of return calcula-
tions.   The economic evaluation of the project uses a sample based on pre-
liminary feasibility studies (RTPs), amounting to about 40% of total pro-
ject investments.  The sample was selected to reflect the subproject types,
investment weights and regional differences expected to prevail under the
project.  No sewerage subprojects from capital cities were included in the
sample because RTPs for those subprojects were unavailable at the time of
appraisal.   Projects  in capital cities  are expected,  however,  to yield
similar results to those in large cities. Besides, overall investments for
sewerage in capital cities are expected to be very small.  The overall re-
turns on the project are deemed to approximate the results obtained from
the sample evaluated.
Least Cost Analysis
5.03        All subprojects in the sample represented the least cost solu-
tion.   Least-cost analysis and subproject  selection criteria,  agreed to
between the Bank and BNH, will be incorporated in all feasibility reports
of subprojects presented to BNH (Annex 5).



- 27 -
Economic Rates of Return and Marginal Cost
5.04       The analysis uses a shadow exchange rate factor of 1.15 and a
shadow labor rate of 0.90. In some communities, some small investments not
related to incremental benefits (such as pipe replacement, maintenance
equipment or studies for future projects) have been excluded from the ana-
lysis.  Economic rates of return were calculated using both covenanted and
present tariff levels as minimum measures for economic benefits.  Summary
results are presented in Table 5.1 and details are presented in Annex 15.
The covenanted tariff levels are considered feasible by the Bank and the
Government.  The rates of return obtained are satisfactory and exceed re-
quirements for subproject selection.  The weighted average rates of return
for the project as a whole are 11% at covenanted and 9% at present tariffs
for water supply, 10% and 7%, for sewerage,6/ and 10% and 8% for water and
sewerage combined. These results are moderately sensitive to increases in
project costs, or decreases in benefits (Table 5.1): a two-year lag in
achieving benefits, for example, would reduce returns by about 2%. Higher
variations than those tested for are unlikely.   Overall rates of return
would more closely approximate or exceed the opportunity cost of capital if
the consumer surplus is included.  Experience shows that, when necessary,
the population is willing to pay much higher prices for smaller volumes of
water. In many cities, 20-30 liters of water purchased from street vendors
by urban poor reach prices 4 to 6 times higher than the present average
tariffs. The rate of return for water supply in capital cities (57% weight
on investments analyzed), including consumer surplus with 10% increase in
costs and 10% decrease in volume sold (Table 5.1), is estimated at about
27%.
