5.05       The marginal costs for water supply and sewerage at an opportu-
nity cost of capital of 11% in July 1982 prices are summarized in Table 5.2
6/ Weighted result for large and medium cities.



- 28 -
and detailed calculations are given in Annex 15.  The consolidated results
for water supply in all four types of communities are satisfactory.
Present average tariffs per cubic meter sold represent about 92% of mar-
ginal cost ranging from 123% in large cities to 56% in small communities.
Covenanted tariffs vary from 149% to 64% of the marginal cost. Since mini-
mum consumption charges are subsidized to make them affordable to the poor
(para. 1.20) and excess consumption is charged at rates about 60%-370%
higher than the minimum charges, the covenanted tariff levels can be consi-
dered  to be  consistent  with marginal  costs.   Results  for sewerage  are
similar.
Population Benefitted and Urban Poverty Impact
5.06      PLANASA investment during 1983-1987 will benefit about 24 million
new consumers with piped water and provide about 12.2 million existing con-
sumers with water improved in quality and quantity. It will increase over-
all urban water supply service levels in Brazil from 69% in 1983 to 89% of
the urban population by 1987.  Sewerage services will be extended to about
nine million individuals, increasing service levels from 30%7/ to 37%.
The impact of the project will continue to increase as complementary works
are completed.  It is roughly estimated that the equivalent number of per-
sons to be served during the total useful life of the project (using an 11%
discount rate) will be about 60 million.
5.07      The direct impact of the program on the poverty group, defined as
families earning a monetary income of three regional minimum salaries or
less8/, in the urban areas of the states was estimated by calculating the
proportion of urban poor in the total incremental urban population served
(Annex 16).   About 78% of the urban poor will have water connections by
1987. About 12 million or about 50% of the new customers to be benefitted
from the water supply investments belong to the poverty group.   Sewerage
investments which previously had been limited to densely populated areas,
will be gradually extended to some medium-sized cities.  About 3.4 million
urban poor (38% of the incremental population to be served) will be in-
cluded among sewerage subproject beneficiaries.   Follow-up sewerage pro-
jects emphasizing low-cost sanitation technologies will be studied during
project execution.
Affordability of Project Services
5.08         The Tariff Law specifies that the minimum monthly charge for
water should not exceed 35% of one ORTN (US$3.95), and the combined charge
for water and sewerage should not exceed 50% of one ORTN (US$5.64).   The
existing monthly charges for minimum consumption of water range from 22% to
78% (average: 50%) of the values allowed by the Tariff Law.  Furthermore,
7/  The changed definition of urban areas has resulted in many rural areas
being classified as urban during the past two years. This has resulted
in a lower overall urban service level from an estimated 74% for water
and 34% for sewerage in 1980.
8/ On an annual basis the three minimum salary threshold corresponds
approximately to one-third of the national per capita income.

minimum water charges are only about 1.6% of the minimum salary.  Connec-
tion charges, which are very low, are being phased out by BNH making water
services accessible to all.   The combined water and sewerage charges are
57% of the values allowed by the Tariff Law and represent only about 2.8%
of the minimum salary.   Under the covenanted tariffs, the minimum water
charges, even if increased in proportion to the average tariff increases,
would remain well below Tariff Law allowances and below 3% of the minimum
salary, which is satisfactory (Annex 16).
Project Risks
5.09       The project would present no significant technical risks since
most of the works to be undertaken do not involve special engineering re-
quirements, and the country has a well developed engineering and construc-
tion industry.   Cost escalations to levels that would jeopardize the pro-
ject's worth are unlikely. Overall the project presents lower than average
risks as only economically sound subprojects and those meeting the selec-
tion criteria would be financed. BNH's supervisory experience gained under
previous projects will be consolidated and improved upon during this pro-
ject. The beneficiaries of the project include several SWCs that have not
been included in previous Bank loans.  Some of these SWCs, as well as some
of those included in previous loans, are still weak.  The project scope is
equivalent to a multiple of more traditional projects in the sector. Bank
supervision efforts would be critical, especially during the first two
years, when BNH's institutional development program is being implemented
and when the new SWCs are being familiarized with Bank procedures. Other-
wise, there would be a substantial risk that the institutional improvements
would not be achieved.
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01       As a condition of loan effectiveness, the first on-lending con-
tract (CTN or CTE) between BNH and three SWCs and States should be signed
(para. 3.12 and L.A. 6.01(b)).
6.02        As a condition for loan disbursement,  the on-lending contract
between BNH and the SWC and State should be signed (para. 3.12 and L.A.
2.02(a)).
6.03         During loan negotiations,  assurances were obtained from the
Federal Government that:
(a)    the Government will provide or cause BNH to provide the required
counterpart funds (para. 4.11 and G.A. 2.01(b) and (c)); and
(b)    the revenue covenant and financial recovery plans will be met
(para. 4.10 and G.A. 3.02(b)).
6.04      During loan negotiations assurances were obtained from BNH that:
(a)   the Bank, the Guarantor and BNH will periodically exchange views
on tariffs (para. 1.19, G.A. 3.03 and L.A. 4.06(c));



- 31 -
(b)   a plan of action satisfactory to the Bank for a national rate
system for sewage collection and treatment services based on a
study prepared under Loan 1525-BR will be presented to the Bank
before September 30, 1983, and promptly implemented in accordance
with an agreed upon schedule (para. 1.20 and L.A. 4.08).
(c)   recommendations made under the technical assistance program for
the monitoring of the SWCs by BNH will be implemented in accord-
ance with an agreed upon schedule (para. 2.04 and L.A. 4.07);
(d)   the technical  assistance  program to BNH will be completed by
September 30, 1984; and views will be periodically exchanged on
the program (para. 2.04 and L.A. 4.07);
(e)   the technical assistance pilot projects will be implemented in
accordance with the EVGs (para. 2.12 and L.A. Schedule 5);
(f)   SWCs internal audit units will  be  established  and  staffed  by
December 31, 1984 (para. 2.14 and L.A. Schedule 5);
(g)   annual external audits of BNH and the SWCs will be made and re-
ports presented to the Bank by April 30 for BNH and May 31 for
the SWCs.   Reports should cover compliance with financial cove-
nants and statements of expenditures (para. 2.15, L.A. 4.04 and
L.A. Schedule 5);
(h)   eligibility criteria for the SWCs to become loan beneficiaries
will include : (i) presentation of an EVG (1983-87) satisfactory
to BNH and the Bank; (ii) presentation of an institutional deve-
lopment program, satisfactory to BNH and the Bank, (iii) a plan
satisfactory to BNH and the Bank, to comply with the Tariff Law
in 1983, or in special cases, to comply by 1985 at the latest;
(iv) compliance with existing loan covenants if already included
in Bank's current projects; and (v) full commitment of loan funds
if already included in Bank's current projects (para. 3.04 and
L.A. 3.01(g));
(i)    BNH will review annually compliance with the on-lending condi-
tions for the SWCs and the states and submit a proposal, satis-
factory to the Bank, including remedial measures and suspension
of new financing of subprojects, if required (para. 3.04 and
L.A. 3.01(h));
(j)    Bank disbursement for subprojects in Minas Gerais, Rio de Janeiro
and Sao Paulo will be limited to 45% of the Bank Loan (para.
3.04 and L.A. 2.02(a));
(k)    all subprojects will meet agreed-upon selection criteria (para.
3.06 and L.A. 3.04);



- 32 -
(1)    the  Bank will  be  given  the  opportunity  to  comment  on  major
changes in the EVGs (para. 3.09 and L.A. 3.06(b));
(m)    terms and conditions, as agreed-upon, will be incorporated in all
on-lending agreements (para. 3.12 and L.A. 3.01(a));
(n)    loan funds will be committed through CTNs or CTEs by December 31,
1986 (para. 3.14 and L.A. 2.03(c));
(o)    semi-annual field supervision of each project SWC will be made
and progress reports will be provided by April 30 and October 31
(para. 3.16 and L.A. 3.03(c));
(p)    project completion report will be prepared within four months of
the loan closing date (para. 3.16 and L.A. 3.03(d));
(q)    the SWCs will follow procurement procedures  in accordance with
Bank guidelines as agreed upon and a general procurement notice
will be up-dated annually (para. 3.18, L.A. 2.04 and Schedule 3);
(r)    BNH will set tariffs in order to cause each project SWC to comply
with the NTL (para. 4.10 and L.A. 4.06);
(s)    counterpart fund requirements will be reviewed each November and
funds will be timely provided (para. 4.11 and L.A. 3.01(c)); and
(t)    monitoring indicators will be prepared by BNH prior to September
30, 1983 (para 4.12 and L.A. 3.07).
6.05        Subject to the above conditions, the project is suitable for a
Bank loan of US$302.3 million to the Banco Nacional da Habitacao for a
period of 15 years including a three year grace period. Retroactive finan-
cing for up to US$15 million for expenditures incurred after January 1,
1983 is also recommended (para. 3.21 and L.A. 2.02 (c)(i)).

ANNEX 4
BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
Credit Lines Under PLANASA
1.         Under the project, BNH would make subloans for approved subpro-
jects of the SWCs using its basic lines of credit (REFINAG for water supply
and REFINESG for sewerage subprojects), as well as its FINEST II and III
programs.  BNH would also provide loans to states for subproject benefit-
ting a high percentage (70%) of the urban poor.  These loans would be pas-
sed to the SWCs on a grant basis. REFINAG and REFINESG provide subloans to
SWCs to cover one-half of the cost of subprojects in medium-size or capital
cities, with the other half of the cost being financed from FAE subloans.
These subloans are made at interest rates of 2.5% to 10.5% per annum on the
outstanding balance which is adjusted for inflation according to the ORTN
index, for terms of 18 years (water supply) to 30 years (sewerage) includ-
ing grace periods of 1.5 to 4.5 years.   BNH under its FINEST II credit
lines provides loans to a state in the same amount that the state has al-
ready passed on as contributions to the SWCs' water and sewerage subpro-
jects in small communities.  Its FINEST III program matches funds provided
by the states to finance specific water or sewerage subprojects in small
communities. The states -- under both FINEST II and III -- transfer BNH's
loan contributions to the beneficiary SWCs to finance the approved subpro-
jects.   For small communities  the SWCs receive 70% of the subprojects'
costs as grants.  Terms of FINEST loans are essentially the same as those
under BNH's REFINAG and REFINESG credit lines, but interest rates are
slightly lower (Attachment 1).

ANNEX 5
Page 1 of 2
BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
Subproject Selection Criteria
1.         All sub-projects should comply with the following conditions to
be acceptable for Bank financing:
(a)  they must have been included in the last EVG approved by BNH and
accepted by the Bank;
(b)  total  subproject cost should not be substantially higher than
that included in the EVG; if subproject scope differs substan-
tially from or costs are more than 30% higher than those contem-
plated in the EVG, satisfactory evidence must be presented to BNH
and the Bank that their inclusion and financing will not dete-
riorate the SWC's financial position; and
(c) subprojects must be the least-cost solution.
2.        Calculations of long-run incremental costs for subprojects and of
present value and long-run incremental cost for least-cost analysis should
use an annual discount rate equal to the opportunity cost of capital (11%)
or another value agreed upon between BNH and the Bank. These calculations
will be based on the subprojects' capital costs, operating costs and com-
plementary investments.
3.        Water supply subprojects to be carried out in cities with popula-
tions greater than 50,000 must either have average water tariffs 1/ equal
to or greater than 75% of the long-run average incremental cost or an IFR
of not less than 5%.
4.          Sewerage subprojects or water supply subprojects to be carried
out in cities with populations between 5,000-50,000 must either have sewer-
age or water tariffs equal to or greater than 60% of the long-run average
incremental cost or an IFR of not less than 1%.
5.          In cases where the IFR is used as the selection criteria, BNH
should in addition prepare a financial analysis of the subproject demon-
strating that the subproject does not have a negative effect on the SWC's
projected cash flows.
1/    The average tariff used is obtained by dividing the revenues from
water (or sewerage) services by the respective volume billed in each
city.  When the present tariff does not satisfy the selection crite-
rion the average tariff (in constant prices) to be implemented within
the following 12 months would be used for a second analysis.



- 41 -
ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 2
6.          Subprojects or group of subprojects to be carried out in com-
munities with populations of less than 5,000 must have a per capita con-
struction cost 2/ of less than US$180 in 1980 prices.
7.   Subprojects not meeting the applicable criterion must be redesigned to
lower acceptable standards, or appropriately phased with due regard to
lowering costs.  If the subproject does not fulfill the criterion after it
has been redesigned, it will be eligible for Bank financing only if a
satisfactory social and economic justification is furnished to the Bank.
The social and economic justification will be made on a case-by-case basis
and will consider, among other things, closeness to the acceptance limits,
percentage of urban poor served, impossibility of providing water by other
means or having additional revenues, health conditions of the population,
and evaluation of benefits on other sectors of the economy.
2/    Construction cost divided by the population to be served after the
project construction is completed.

BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
List of Conditions for Onlending Agreements
A project loan agreement (CTN or CTE) signed by BNH and each of
the states and SWCs would be used for each subproject to be financed. This
agreement will refer to general conditions and commitments contained in the
Loan Agreement. A list of general conditions follows:
Obligations of the Project States
1.        support to the project; and
2.        agreement on the EVG and commitment  to finance its investment
program.
Obligations of the Project Companies
1.        carry out the project;
2.        hire qualified consultants;
3.        arrange adequate insurance coverage;
4.        make adequate use of goods and services being financed;
5.        prepare and timely deliver to BNH the project documentation;
6.        maintain satisfactory project records;
7.        authorize visits of BNH and Bank personnel to works and offfices
of the SWCs;
8.        prepare progress and completion reports;
9.        comply with subproject selection criteria;
10.       maintain satisfactory accounting records;



- 52 -
ANNEX 7
Page 2 of 2
11.       maintain external audit;
12.       revise each year the EVG for a five-year period;
13.       carry out the institutional development program as detailed in
the current EVG, including programs for reduction of unaccounted
for water and for studies of tariff structures;
14.       carry out the investment program set forth in the EVG;
15.       comply with National Tariff Law;
16.       set up an internal audit unit by December 31, 1984;
17.       comply with procurement guidelines; and
18.       execute agreed recovery plans aimed at strengthening the com-
pany's financial situation.

BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
Procurement
Procurement guidelines
1.         In mid-1981, BNH issued procurement guidelines for use in Bank-
financed projects to complement Bank guidelines.   BNH guidelines   detail
supervision of procurement processes by BNH and the Bank and include the
stages of project preparation and review from the EVG to the disbursement
of Bank loan funds  (Attachment  1 to this Annex).   The guidelines were
amended at the end of 1981 to give BNH more responsibility for these mat-
ters, reducing Bank supervision to ex post review of most documents.  The
Bank performs a prior review of the onlending contracts among BNH, SWC and
State. These documents will include general lending conditions and commit-
ments detailed in Annex 7, summary description of works or goods, costs,
and execution schedule.  Together with the CTN, the Bank reviews the com-
pliance of the subproject with the selection criteria agreed upon (Annex
5).
Bidding Documents
2.          BNH has prepared standard general conditions for bidding docu-
ments to be used by SWCs in all tenders. Drafts of these documents are be-
ing reviewed by the Bank's Legal Department.   BNH would accept slight de-
viations requested by individual states. These amendments would be subject
to ex post review by the Bank. BNH has also issued guidelines for grouping
goods and works in bidding packages to make them more attractive for inter-
national competitive bidding.

ANNEX 10

BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
Disbursement Procedures
Introduction
1.          As in previous loans to BNH, disbursement will be made against
statements of expenditures.  This is a practical procedure for a loan made
to a financial intermediary, with a large number of subprojects and with
contracts generally awarded to local contractors.  The disbursement proce-
dure is consistent with BNH's procedures, allows proper control over Bank
disbursements and is working satisfactorily in ongoing projects. BNH's fi-
nancing mechanism and Bank reimbursement procedures are described below.
BNH/SWC Financing Contract
2.         After BNH has approved designs for any subproject, the SWC sub-
mits a request for PLANASA financing to BNH's financial agent in the
state.   The financial agent then submits the request to BNH and to the
FAE. The parties then sign formal loan agreements establishing conditions,
the total amount of the loan, amount of the advance payment and repayment
terms.  For control purposes, BNH establishes a financial contract number
to each contract. All subsequent payment requests must bear that number.
Borrower's (BNH) Expenditure Control Procedure
3.         BNH examines all bidding documents and final contracts to verify
that they pertain to the subproject. At the state level, SOSAN and the OTs
review all invoices to establish their validity as to percentage of comple-
tion and delivery of materials, and also monitor the construction of all
subprojects.   The financial agent compares the authenticity of documenta-
tion and verifies paid invoices with advance payment requests.
BNH Disbursements
4.        BNH disburses in two stages:  (a) an initial advance of up to 20%
of the total expenditure stipulated in the financing contract, and (b) sub-
sequent payments until the project is completed.   Subsequent payments may
be made against paid or unpaid invoices.



- 57 -
ANNEX 10
Page 2 of 2
5.        The initial advance is paid to the financial agent who credits it
to the SWC's account after deducting the administrative and technical ser-
vice fees. The advance is recovered by deducting from each subsequent pay-
ment the percentage of the total contract that the advance represented. If
there are overruns, the advance is automatically liquidated.  Overruns are
generally financed by a supplementary financial contract.
6.          The SWC has two payment options.  Under the first alternative,
the SWC submits paid invoices to the OT who approves (or rejects) each in-
voice, and returns all documents to the SWC.  The SWC then submits a list
of the approved invoices to the financial agent who verifies and submits
the list and a request for reimbursement to BNH and FAE. Under the second
alternative, the procedure is identical except that a list of unpaid in-
voices is submitted and the request is for advance payment, supported by
proof that the previous month's advance was used to pay the invoices.
7.        BNH issues a payment order for 50% of the value of the request to
which the list of paid invoices is attached.   The request is approved by
COSAN, and then routed to BNH's Finance Department for payment through the
financial agent.   Administrative and technical service fee are deducted.
BNH exercises satisfactory control over subproject implementation and dis-
bursements procedures.   The accounting procedures are straightforward and
records are available for supervision missions to verify expenditures on
selected subprojects.   Furthermore, independent auditors audit the state-
ments of expenditure and the associated internal controls.
Transactions between BNH, the final user and the Bank
8.        Contract amounts, disbursements and payment of interest and prin-
cipal are expressed in UPC.   The UPC is a monetary unit adjusted every
three months in accordance with the percentage increase granted to Federal
Government Bonds (ORTN).  BNH submits to the Bank requests for reimburse-
ments for expenditures on approved subprojects (CTNs or CTEs) supported by
the list of paid or unpaid invoices.  In order to reduce the interval dur-
ing which BNH has to finance the Bank's share of project cost with its own
resources, and the underdisbursement of the Bank loan under high local in-
flation rates, BNH and the Bank agreed to make advance payments in the
amount of US$20 million from the Loan account into a Special Account in the
Banco Central do Brasil.  The Special Account would be available for fi-
nancing only the Bank's share of project cost.  BNH would be entitled to
make withdrawals from the Special Account at the exchange rate applicable
on the day the BNH made its disbursement.

ANNEX 13
Page 1 of 2
BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
SWCs' Summary Financial Data
Assumptions
1.         The EVGs form the basis for the financial projections and moni-
toring indicators. The EVGs provide a five year planning horizon and in-
clude, inter alia, past and projected service levels, investment programs
to meet these service levels, monitoring indicators and detailed financial
projections (balance sheet and income and cash flow statements). The EVGs
reviewed cover the years 1983-87 and include historical and current data
for 1981 and 1982.  The mission reviewed the EVGs of 23 SWCs.  Three SWCs
had not presented their EVGs at the time of appraisal.   Several modifica-
tions were made in the assumptions underlying the EVGs.
2.         The UPC is used as the monetary unit in the EVGs  because of the
high inflation rate experienced in Cruzeiros.   In 1979 and 1980, adjust-
ments to the UPC lagged considerably below the inflation rate. Since 1981,
the Government has maintained a policy of fully adjusting the UPC with in-
flation. This policy is expected to be continued.
3.        Volumes billed are based on projected service levels contained in
the EVGs. Average tariffs are based on the requirements of the National
Tariff Law (sum of operating costs, depreciation and debt service) and in-
clude tariff structure changes and acceleration of billing cycles for
several of the companies.
4.           Unaccounted-for water is projected to decrease 10% p.a. to a
minimum level of 20% in those SWCs with unaccounted-for water higher than
20% in 1982.   In those SWCs with reported unaccounted-for water of less
than 20%, no decreases are assumed.
5.        Inflation rates are estimated at 8% in 1983, 7.5% in 1984, 7% in
1985 and 6% p.a. thereafter.
6.          Operating costs are projected by applying the general inflation
factors and certain assumptions, as follows:
personnel - gradual reductions in the number of employees per one
thousand water and sewer connections;
chemicals and power - increases in direct proportion to the in-
creases in water production; and
materials and other - increases in direct proportion to the in-
creases in the number of water connections.



- 63 -
ANNEX 13
Page 2 of 2
7.             Current assets  and liabilities  are based on the following
assumptions:
accounts receivable - gradual reduction to two months of sales in
receivables for those companies with greater than two months sales as of
December 31, 1981.  In those SWCs with less than two months sales, no re-
ductions are assumed;
inventories - 1-1/2 months of operating costs less personnel and
power expenses;
other current assets, accounts payable and other current liabili-
ties - estimated December 31, 1982 value per the EVG adjusted annually for
inflation.

BRAZIL
WATER SUPPLY AND SEWERAGE SECTOR PROJECT
Economic and Social Analysis
Economic Rate of Return (ERR) and Marginal Cost Calculations
1.         The sample of investment projects analyzed represents about 40%
of total project costs and was selected from studies (RTPs) that were
ready at appraisal. The sample covers about 13 states and reflects project
types, investment weights, and regional differences expected to prevail in
future investments under the project. No sewerage subprojects from capital
cities were included in the sample because RTPs for those subprojects were
unavailable at appraisal.  Subprojects in capital cities are, however, ex-
pected to yield similar results to those in large cities. Overall invest-
ments for sewerage in capital cities are expected to be very small.  Eco-
nomic rates of return (ERR) have been calcilated for water, sewerage, and
water and sewerage for subprojects consolic&ted on the basis of rommu.nity
size (capital, large and medium cities, and sma 1 c)mmunit3, (At-achment.,
1-5).   ERRs were also calculated for individual subprojects  (details in
project files).  The ERR was estimated for consolidated water and sewerage
investments in large and medium cities at present and covenanted tariffs,
and for the project as a whole.   The results and weighted averages are
shown in Table 5.1.  The marginal cost for the investments has been esti-
mated as the average long term incremental cost of the project investment
and complementary investments.  The incremental values of the consolidated
samples are summarized in Table 5.2, and further details are given in this
Annex and the Project File. The analysis of the marginal cost and ERR have
been estimated separately for individual project investments in the sample
for consolidated projects based on community types and for the consolidated
samples of water supply, sewerage, and water and sewerage investments.
2           Revenues generated by the sample projects have been used as a
proxv for economic benefits.   Present tariffs used in the anlaysis were
those -f July 1982.  Covenanted tariffs (levels of tariffs required by the
financial covenant) were expressed in July 1982 prices.  Although the same
tariff structure is used for all cities within a SWC, the average tariff is
always higher in the capital and large cities because of the effect on the
average of industrial and commercial users, and of the higher proportion of
large residential users who purchase water at excess consumption charges (2
to 2.5 times than for minimum consumption).   In the samples analyzed, the



- 80 -
ANNEX 15
Page 2 of 3
average tariffs in medium cities appear higher than those in large and
capital cities.   This may be explained by the effect of high tariffs in
some states from which the medium cities samples were selected. Severage
charges for the most part are between 80-100% of water tariffs.
3.            Traditionally, ERRs are not calculated for sewerage projects
alone since charges are related to the volume of water used, and it is not
possible for consumers to indicate their willingness to pay for each ser-
vice independently, and because a large proportion of the population lacks
sewerage services.  Furthermore, there are no rates for sewage treatment.
The reason why ERR calculations for sewerage alone are presented here is
that it was possible to separate revenue receipts for sewerage.   The re-
suits severely underestimate benefits.   All investments are expressed in
July 1982 UPC and include engineering and physical contingencies. Foreign
exchange has been shadow priced at 1.15 times the official rate of ex-
change.   The labor shadow price was set at 0.9 times the average wagee
Project investments, as well as complementary investments to be made by the
SWCs during and after project execution, have been included in the analy-
sis.  Complementary investments are mainly house connections, which would
be largely financed by urban developers, contributions to construction (BNH
urban poverty housing programs) and some connection fees. Treated water in
many cities will be substituted for water being provided without or with
partial treatment.
Selection Criteria
4.           The justification of each individual subproject will be made
using either the long-run average incremental cost (marginal cost) calcu-
lated on the basis of the investment program of the project and its comple-
mentary works, or the internal financial rate of return (IFR) (Annex 5).
Projects in which the present tariff or that to be implemented in the near
future exceeds 75% of the marginal cost or those with an IFR of 5% I/ or
more would be approved.   Projects not meeting these criteria will be de-
signed to standards affordable to the population. Such projects would also
only be approved if their impact on the incidence of poverty or health im-
provement would be substantial and if there is evidence that such projects
could contribute to the overall economic growth of the areas to be served.
The use of marginal cost simplifies project selection, provides useful in-
sights of cost structures, and is easy for policy makers to understand. On
the other hand, the use of the IFR also provides sector institutions addi-
tional flexibility to approve projects that have satisfactory returns on
investment subject to the preparation of financial analyses of the projects
and their effects on the SWCs based on projected cash flows.   For small
communities, investment per capita costs would be used as the selection
1/ 60% for sewerage, or water supply projects in cities with less than
RN, nnn .           (-  -r  m  1 / TV 8



- 81 -
ANNEX 15
Page 3 of 3
criterion and would be applied to subprojects or groups of subprojects car-
ried out in such communities.   Marginal costs will give very useful sta-
tistics on per capita investment costs, operating expenses and the cities
where tariff surcharges and intensive metering should be applied.
Economic Rate of Return including Consumer Surplus
5.        The ERRs, whether at present or covenanted tariffs measure only a
part of a project's benefits. The results of the EER can get closer to the
full economic return if the consumer surplus is included.   The consumer
surplus can be estimated if the price elasticity is known.   Two points on
the demand curve are known: the average charges (Y1) and consumption (X1),
and the price paid by urban poor to water vendors (YO) for very small
amount of water (XO).  An exponential function of the form Y=Ce-bx can be
fitted between these points. The ratio (k) between the social and economic
benefits perceived by the users and the ERR will be the area below this
function divided by the benefits estimated by the traditional ERR (X1, mul-
tiplied by Y1). The integration of Y= Ce-bx between Xo and X1 provides the
Area A=(X0-X1) (YO-Y1)/ln(YI/YO). The project benefits, including consumer
surplus, are then K times higher than those measured by the ERR.
K = (Y1-YO)/(Y1ln(Y1/YO) = (l-YO/YL)/ln(Y1/YO)
6.         Urban poor purchase small amounts of water (20 liters per capita
per day or about 2-3 m3 per family per month) at prices 4 to 6 times the
average price for piped water. For a ratio of Yo to Y1 of 4, k = 2.20, and
the consumer surplus can be estimated as the ERR at a tariff equal to 2.2
times the average tariff.   The weighted average rate of return for water
supply in the capital cities sample including the estimated consumer sur-
plus, is included in Table 5.1.
Urban Poverty
7.         The levels of urban poverty, defined as families earning a mone-
tary income of three regional minimum salaries or less, were estimated by
the appraisal team (Annex 16) using the information available from the 1980
census results and other previous household surveys conducted by the IBGE.
The number of urban poor presently served is generally low because the
overall service levels are low (for water supply, 69%) and persons with
higher income usually receive piped water first.   The analysis of incre-
mental population for the sample is in the project file and for the project
as a whole, in Annex 16.  The impact on poverty and affordability of pro-
ject services is also detailed in Annex 16.
